The <a href="/wiki/Chevrolet_Sequel" title="Chevrolet Sequel">Chevrolet Sequel</a> hydrogen fuel cell-powered concept <a href="/wiki/SUV" class="mw-redirect" title="SUV">SUV</a> vehicle</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>As of 2016<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Hydrogen_vehicle&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, there are 3 hydrogen cars publicly available in select markets; the <a href="/wiki/Toyota_Mirai" title="Toyota Mirai">Toyota Mirai</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Hyundai_ix35_FCEV" title="Hyundai ix35 FCEV">Hyundai ix35 FCEV</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Honda_Clarity" class="mw-redirect" title="Honda Clarity">Honda Clarity</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ihs2016_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ihs2016-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>Toyota launched its first production fuel cell vehicle, the <a href="/wiki/Toyota_Mirai" title="Toyota Mirai">Mirai</a>, in Japan at the end of 2014 and began sales in California, mainly the <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles_metropolitan_area" title="Los Angeles metropolitan area">Los Angeles area</a>, in 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-Voelcker_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voelcker-11">[11]</a></sup> The car has a range of 312&#160;mi (502&#160;km) and takes about five minutes to refill its hydrogen tank. The initial sale price in Japan was about 7 million yen ($69,000).<sup id="cite_ref-bloomberg.com_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bloomberg.com-12">[12]</a></sup> Former European Parliament President <a href="/wiki/Pat_Cox" title="Pat Cox">Pat Cox</a> estimates that Toyota will initially lose about $100,000 on each Mirai sold.<sup id="cite_ref-Cost2014_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cost2014-13">[13]</a></sup> Many automobile companies have introduced demonstration models in limited numbers (see <a href="/wiki/List_of_fuel_cell_vehicles" title="List of fuel cell vehicles">list of fuel cell vehicles</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-Whoriskey_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Whoriskey-14">[14]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> One disadvantage of hydrogen compared to other automobile fuels is its low density.<sup id="cite_ref-lanz_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lanz-16">[16]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-zubrin121_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-zubrin121-17">[17]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-olah155_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-olah155-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<p>Several manufacturers are collaborating on hydrogen technology development, with 2013 announcements of <a href="/wiki/BMW" title="BMW">BMW</a> leasing technology from <a href="/wiki/Toyota" title="Toyota">Toyota</a>, and another group formed by <a href="/wiki/Ford_Motor_Company" title="Ford Motor Company">Ford Motor Company</a>, <a href="/wiki/Daimler_AG" title="Daimler AG">Daimler AG</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Nissan" title="Nissan">Nissan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Buses">Buses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydrogen_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Buses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Fuel_cell_bus" title="Fuel cell bus">fuel cell bus</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:TOYOTA_FCHV_Bus.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/TOYOTA_FCHV_Bus.jpg/220px-TOYOTA_FCHV_Bus.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/TOYOTA_FCHV_Bus.jpg/330px-TOYOTA_FCHV_Bus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/TOYOTA_FCHV_Bus.jpg/440px-TOYOTA_FCHV_Bus.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1280" data-file-height="960" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:TOYOTA_FCHV_Bus.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Toyota_FCHV-BUS" class="mw-redirect" title="Toyota FCHV-BUS">Toyota FCHV-BUS</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Expo_2005" title="Expo 2005">Expo 2005</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Fuel cell buses (as opposed to hydrogen fueled buses) are being <a href="/wiki/Fuel_cell_bus_trial" class="mw-redirect" title="Fuel cell bus trial">trialed</a> by several manufacturers in different locations. The <a href="/wiki/Fuel_Cell_Bus_Club" title="Fuel Cell Bus Club">Fuel Cell Bus Club</a> is a global fuel cell bus testing collaboration.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Trams">Trams</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydrogen_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Trams">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In March 2015, <a href="/wiki/CSR_Corporation_Limited" title="CSR Corporation Limited">China South Rail Corporation</a> (CSR) demonstrated the world's first hydrogen fuel cell-powered tramcar at an assembly facility in Qingdao. The chief engineer of the CSR subsidiary <a href="/wiki/CSR_Sifang_Co_Ltd." title="CSR Sifang Co Ltd.">CSR Sifang Co Ltd.</a>, Liang Jianying, said that the company is studying how to reduce the running costs of the tram.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> A total of 83 miles of tracks for the new vehicle have been built in seven Chinese cities. China plans to spend 200 billion yuan ($32 billion) over the next five years to increase tram tracks to more than 1,200 miles.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bicycles">Bicycles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydrogen_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Bicycles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/PHB_(bicycle)" title="PHB (bicycle)">PHB (bicycle)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hydrogen_bicycle.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Hydrogen_bicycle.jpg/220px-Hydrogen_bicycle.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Hydrogen_bicycle.jpg/330px-Hydrogen_bicycle.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Hydrogen_bicycle.jpg/440px-Hydrogen_bicycle.jpg 2x" data-file-width="960" data-file-height="720" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hydrogen_bicycle.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/PHB_(bicycle)" title="PHB (bicycle)">Hydrogen bicycle</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Pearl Hydrogen Power Sources of <a href="/wiki/Shanghai,_China" class="mw-redirect" title="Shanghai, China">Shanghai, China</a>, unveiled a hydrogen bicycle at the 9th China International Exhibition on Gas Technology, Equipment and Applications in 2007.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Motorcycles_and_scooters">Motorcycles and scooters</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydrogen_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Motorcycles and scooters">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/ENV" title="ENV">ENV</a> develops electric motorcycles powered by a hydrogen fuel cell, including the <a href="/wiki/Crosscage" class="mw-redirect" title="Crosscage">Crosscage</a> and <a href="/w/index.php?title=Biplane_(motorcycle)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Biplane (motorcycle) (page does not exist)">Biplane</a>. Other manufacturers as Vectrix are working on hydrogen scooters.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> Finally, hydrogen fuel cell-electric hybrid scooters are being made such as the <a href="/wiki/Suzuki_Burgman" title="Suzuki Burgman">Suzuki Burgman fuel cell scooter</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> and the <a href="/w/index.php?title=FHybrid&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="FHybrid (page does not exist)">FHybrid</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> The Burgman received "whole vehicle type" approval in the EU.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> The Taiwanese company APFCT conducted a live street test with 80 fuel cell scooters for Taiwans Bureau of Energy.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Quads_and_tractors">Quads and tractors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydrogen_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Quads and tractors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/w/index.php?title=Autostudi_S.r.l&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Autostudi S.r.l (page does not exist)">Autostudi S.r.l</a>'s H-Due<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> is a hydrogen-powered quad, capable of transporting 1-3 passengers. A concept for a hydrogen powered tractor has been proposed.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Airplanes">Airplanes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydrogen_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Airplanes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">For more details on this topic, see <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_planes" class="mw-redirect" title="Hydrogen planes">Hydrogen planes</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Boeing_Fuel_Cell_Demonstrator_AB1.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Boeing_Fuel_Cell_Demonstrator_AB1.JPG/220px-Boeing_Fuel_Cell_Demonstrator_AB1.JPG" width="220" height="144" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Boeing_Fuel_Cell_Demonstrator_AB1.JPG/330px-Boeing_Fuel_Cell_Demonstrator_AB1.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Boeing_Fuel_Cell_Demonstrator_AB1.JPG/440px-Boeing_Fuel_Cell_Demonstrator_AB1.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3738" data-file-height="2453" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Boeing_Fuel_Cell_Demonstrator_AB1.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Boeing" title="Boeing">Boeing</a> Fuel Cell Demonstrator powered by a hydrogen fuel cell</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Companies such as <a href="/wiki/Boeing" title="Boeing">Boeing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lange_Aviation" title="Lange Aviation">Lange Aviation</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/German_Aerospace_Center" title="German Aerospace Center">German Aerospace Center</a> pursue hydrogen as fuel for manned and unmanned airplanes. In February 2008 Boeing tested a manned flight of a small aircraft powered by a hydrogen fuel cell. Unmanned hydrogen planes have also been tested.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> For large passenger airplanes however, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Times" title="The Times">The Times</a></i> reported that "Boeing said that hydrogen fuel cells were unlikely to power the engines of large passenger jet airplanes but could be used as backup or auxiliary power units onboard."<sup id="cite_ref-Apr08Times_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Apr08Times-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<p>In July 2010 Boeing unveiled its hydrogen powered <a href="/wiki/Boeing_Phantom_Eye" title="Boeing Phantom Eye">Phantom Eye</a> <a href="/wiki/Unmanned_aerial_vehicle" title="Unmanned aerial vehicle">UAV</a>, powered by two Ford internal combustion engines that have been converted to run on hydrogen.<sup id="cite_ref-The_Register_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Register-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<p>In Britain, the <a href="/wiki/Reaction_Engines_A2" title="Reaction Engines A2">Reaction Engines A2</a> has been proposed to use the thermodynamic properties of <a href="/wiki/Liquid_hydrogen" title="Liquid hydrogen">liquid hydrogen</a> to achieve very high speed, long distance (antipodal) flight by burning it in a <a href="/wiki/Precooled_jet_engine" title="Precooled jet engine">precooled jet engine</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fork_trucks">Fork trucks</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydrogen_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Fork trucks">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A HICE forklift or HICE lift truck is a hydrogen fueled, <a href="/wiki/Internal_combustion_engine" title="Internal combustion engine">internal combustion engine</a>-powered industrial <a href="/wiki/Forklift_truck" class="mw-redirect" title="Forklift truck">forklift truck</a> used for lifting and <a href="/wiki/Transport" title="Transport">transporting</a> materials. The first production HICE forklift truck based on the Linde X39 Diesel was presented at an exposition in <a href="/wiki/Hannover" class="mw-redirect" title="Hannover">Hannover</a> on May 27, 2008. It used a 2.0 litre, 43&#160;kW (58&#160;hp) diesel internal combustion engine converted to use hydrogen as a fuel with the use of a compressor and <a href="/wiki/Fuel_injection#Direct_injection" title="Fuel injection">direct injection</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-HyICE_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HyICE-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Fuel_cell_forklift" title="Fuel cell forklift">fuel cell forklift</a> (also called a fuel cell lift truck or a fuel cell forklift) is a fuel cell powered industrial forklift truck. In 2013 there were over 4,000 fuel cell forklifts used in <a href="/wiki/Material_handling" title="Material handling">material handling</a> in the US.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> Only 500 of these received funding from <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Energy" title="United States Department of Energy">DOE</a> in 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> The global market is 1 million fork lifts per year.<sup id="cite_ref-Report_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Report-37">[37]</a></sup> As of 2013<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Hydrogen_vehicle&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, fuel cell fleets are being operated by several of companies, including <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sysco_Foods&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Sysco Foods (page does not exist)">Sysco Foods</a>, <a href="/wiki/FedEx" title="FedEx">FedEx</a> Freight, <a href="/wiki/GENCO" class="mw-redirect" title="GENCO">GENCO</a> (at Wegmans, Coca-Cola, <a href="/wiki/Kimberly-Clark" title="Kimberly-Clark">Kimberly-Clark</a>, and Whole Foods), and H-E-B Grocers.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> A total of 30 fuel cell forklifts with Hylift were demonstrated in Europe and extended it with HyLIFT-EUROPE to 200 units.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> With other projects in France<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Austria" title="Austria">Austria</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> Pike Research stated in 2011 that fuel-cell-powered forklifts will be the largest driver of hydrogen fuel demand by 2020.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<p>Most companies in Europe and the US do not use petroleum powered forklifts, as these vehicles work indoors where emissions must be controlled and instead use electric forklifts.<sup id="cite_ref-Report_37-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Report-37">[37]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> Fuel-cell-powered forklifts can provide benefits over battery powered forklifts as they can work for a full 8-hour shift on a single tank of hydrogen and can be refueled in 3 minutes. Fuel cell-powered forklifts can be used in refrigerated warehouses, as their performance is not degraded by lower temperatures. The FC units are often designed as drop-in replacements.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rockets">Rockets</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydrogen_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Rockets">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Many large <a href="/wiki/Rocket" title="Rocket">rockets</a> use <a href="/wiki/Liquid_hydrogen" title="Liquid hydrogen">liquid hydrogen</a> as fuel, with <a href="/wiki/Liquid_oxygen" title="Liquid oxygen">liquid oxygen</a> as an oxidizer. An advantage of hydrogen rocket fuel is the high <a href="/wiki/Effective_exhaust_velocity" class="mw-redirect" title="Effective exhaust velocity">effective exhaust velocity</a> compared to <a href="/wiki/Kerosene" title="Kerosene">kerosene</a>/<a href="/wiki/LOX" class="mw-redirect" title="LOX">LOX</a> or <a href="/wiki/UDMH" class="mw-redirect" title="UDMH">UDMH</a>/<a href="/wiki/Nitrogen_tetroxide" class="mw-redirect" title="Nitrogen tetroxide">NTO</a> engines. According to the <a href="/wiki/Tsiolkovsky_rocket_equation" title="Tsiolkovsky rocket equation">Tsiolkovsky rocket equation</a>, a rocket with higher exhaust velocity needs less propellant mass to achieve a given change of speed. Before combustion, the hydrogen runs through cooling pipes around the <a href="/wiki/Rocket_engine_nozzle" title="Rocket engine nozzle">exhaust nozzle</a> to protect the nozzle from damage by the hot exhaust gases.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> Also the energy content or <a href="/wiki/Energy_density" title="Energy density">energy density</a> of hydrogen calculated from weight is the best compared to any other chemical energy storage.<sup id="cite_ref-H2_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-H2-48">[48]</a></sup> In combination with an oxidizer such as liquid oxygen, liquid hydrogen yields the highest specific impulse, or efficiency in relation to the amount of propellant consumed, of any known rocket propellant.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup></p>
<p>A disadvantage of LH2/LOX engines are the low density and low temperature of liquid hydrogen, which means bigger and insulated and thus heavier fuel tanks are needed. This increases the rocket's structural mass which reduces its delta-v significantly. Another disadvantage is the poor storability of LH2/LOX-powered rockets: Due to the constant hydrogen boil-off, the rocket can only be fueled shortly before launch, which makes cryogenic engines unsuitable for <a href="/wiki/ICBM" class="mw-redirect" title="ICBM">ICBMs</a> and other rocket applications with the need for short launch preparations.</p>
<p>Overall, the delta-v of a hydrogen stage is typically not much different from that of a dense fuelled stage, however, the weight of a hydrogen stage is much less, which makes it particularly effective for upper stages, since they are carried by the lower stages. For first stages, dense fuelled rockets in studies may show a small advantage, due to the smaller vehicle size and lower air drag.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<p>Liquid hydrogen and oxygen were also used in the <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle" title="Space Shuttle">Space Shuttle</a> to run the fuel cells that power the electrical systems.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> The byproduct of the fuel cell is water, which is used for drinking and other applications that require water in space.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Internal_combustion_vehicle">Internal combustion vehicle</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydrogen_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Internal combustion vehicle">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_internal_combustion_engine_vehicle" title="Hydrogen internal combustion engine vehicle">Hydrogen internal combustion engine vehicle</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_hydrogen_internal_combustion_engine_vehicles" title="List of hydrogen internal combustion engine vehicles">List of hydrogen internal combustion engine vehicles</a></div>
<p>Hydrogen internal combustion engine cars are different from hydrogen fuel cell cars. The <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_internal_combustion_engine_vehicle" title="Hydrogen internal combustion engine vehicle">hydrogen internal combustion car</a> is a slightly modified version of the traditional gasoline <a href="/wiki/Internal_combustion_engine" title="Internal combustion engine">internal combustion engine</a> car. These hydrogen engines burn fuel in the same manner that gasoline engines do; the main difference is the exhaust product. Gasoline combustion results in <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> and <a href="/wiki/Water_vapour" class="mw-redirect" title="Water vapour">water vapour</a>, while the only exhaust product of hydrogen combustion is water vapour.</p>
<p>In 1807 <a href="/wiki/Francois_Isaac_de_Rivaz" class="mw-redirect" title="Francois Isaac de Rivaz">Francois Isaac de Rivaz</a> designed <a href="/wiki/De_Rivaz_engine" title="De Rivaz engine">the first hydrogen-fueled internal combustion engine</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> In 1965, <a href="/wiki/Roger_Billings" class="mw-redirect" title="Roger Billings">Roger Billings</a>, then a high school student, converted a <a href="/wiki/Ford_Model_A_(1927%E2%80%931931)" class="mw-redirect" title="Ford Model A (1927–1931)">Model A</a> to run on hydrogen.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> In 1970 Paul Dieges patented a modification to internal combustion engines which allowed a gasoline-powered engine to run on hydrogen <span class="citation patent"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://worldwide.espacenet.com/textdoc?DB=EPODOC&amp;IDX=US3844262">US 3844262</a></span><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Apatent&amp;rft.number=3844262&amp;rft.cc=US&amp;rft.title="><span style="display: none;">&#160;</span></span>.</p>
<p>Mazda has developed <a href="/wiki/Wankel_engine" title="Wankel engine">Wankel engines</a> burning hydrogen.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The advantage of using an internal combustion engine, like Wankel and piston engines, is the lower cost of retooling for production.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/HICE_forklift_truck" class="mw-redirect" title="HICE forklift truck">HICE forklift trucks</a> have been demonstrated<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> based on converted diesel internal combustion engines with <a href="/wiki/Fuel_injection#Direct_injection" title="Fuel injection">direct injection</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-HyICE_33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HyICE-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Fuel_cell">Fuel cell</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydrogen_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Fuel cell">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">For more details on this topic, see <a href="/wiki/Fuel_cell" title="Fuel cell">Fuel cell</a>.</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fuel_cell_cost">Fuel cell cost</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydrogen_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Fuel cell cost">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Hydrogen fuel cells are relatively expensive to produce, as their designs require rare substances such as <a href="/wiki/Platinum" title="Platinum">platinum</a> as a <a href="/wiki/Catalyst" class="mw-redirect" title="Catalyst">catalyst</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-RSC2_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RSC2-55">[55]</a></sup> In 2014, Toyota said it would introduce its <a href="/wiki/Toyota_Mirai" title="Toyota Mirai">Toyota Mirai</a> in Japan for less than $70,000 in 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-bloomberg.com_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bloomberg.com-12">[12]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Toyotapresentation_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Toyotapresentation-56">[56]</a></sup> Former European Parliament President <a href="/wiki/Pat_Cox" title="Pat Cox">Pat Cox</a> estimates that Toyota will initially lose about $100,000 on each Mirai sold.<sup id="cite_ref-Cost2014_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cost2014-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Freezing_conditions">Freezing conditions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydrogen_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Freezing conditions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The problems in early fuel cell designs at low temperatures concerning range and cold start capabilities have been addressed so that they "cannot be seen as show-stoppers anymore".<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> Users in 2014 said that their fuel cell vehicles perform flawlessly in temperatures below zero, even with the heaters blasting, without significantly reducing range.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Service_life">Service life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydrogen_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Service life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The service life of fuel cells is comparable to that of other vehicles.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> PEM <a href="/wiki/Service_life" title="Service life">service life</a> is 7,300 hours under cycling conditions.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Hydrogen">Hydrogen</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydrogen_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Hydrogen">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Hydrogen does not come as a pre-existing source of <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">energy</a> like <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">fossil fuels</a>, but is first produced and then stored as a carrier,<sup id="cite_ref-zmorris_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-zmorris-61">[61]</a></sup> much like a <a href="/wiki/Battery_(electricity)" title="Battery (electricity)">battery</a>. A suggested benefit of large-scale deployment of hydrogen vehicles is that it could lead to decreased emissions of greenhouse gases and ozone precursors.<sup id="cite_ref-Schultz_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schultz-62">[62]</a></sup> However, as of 2014, 95% of hydrogen is made from methane. It can be produced using renewable sources, but that is an expensive process.<sup id="cite_ref-Production_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Production-3">[3]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> Integrated wind-to-hydrogen (<a href="/wiki/Power_to_gas" title="Power to gas">power to gas</a>) plants, using <a href="/wiki/Electrolysis_of_water" title="Electrolysis of water">electrolysis of water</a>, are exploring technologies to deliver costs low enough, and quantities great enough, to compete with traditional energy sources.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to Ford Motor Company, "when FCVs are run on hydrogen reformed from natural gas using this process, they do not provide significant environmental benefits on a well-to-wheels basis (due to GHG emissions from the natural gas reformation process)."<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> While methods of hydrogen production that do not use fossil fuel would be more sustainable,<sup id="cite_ref-Kreith_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kreith-66">[66]</a></sup> currently renewable energy represents only a small percentage of energy generated, and power produced from renewable sources can be used in electric vehicles and for non-vehicle applications.<sup id="cite_ref-TechRev_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TechRev-67">[67]</a></sup></p>
<p>The challenges facing the use of hydrogen in vehicles include production, storage, transport and distribution. The well-to-wheel efficiency for hydrogen is less than 25%.<sup id="cite_ref-Cox2014_7-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cox2014-7">[7]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-EFCF_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EFCF-68">[68]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-EnergyCollective_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EnergyCollective-70">[70]</a></sup> A study sponsored by the U.S. Department of Energy said in 2004 that the well-to-wheel efficiency of gasoline or diesel powered vehicles is even less.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Production">Production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydrogen_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">For more details on this topic, see <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_production" title="Hydrogen production">Hydrogen production</a>.</div>
<p>The molecular hydrogen needed as an on-board fuel for hydrogen vehicles can be obtained through many thermochemical methods utilizing <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">natural gas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Coal" title="Coal">coal</a> (by a process known as coal gasification), <a href="/wiki/Liquefied_petroleum_gas" title="Liquefied petroleum gas">liquefied petroleum gas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Biomass" title="Biomass">biomass</a> (<a href="/wiki/Biomass_gasification" class="mw-redirect" title="Biomass gasification">biomass gasification</a>), by a process called <a href="/wiki/Thermolysis" class="mw-redirect" title="Thermolysis">thermolysis</a>, or as a microbial waste product called <a href="/wiki/Biohydrogen" title="Biohydrogen">biohydrogen</a> or <a href="/wiki/Biological_hydrogen_production_(Algae)" class="mw-redirect" title="Biological hydrogen production (Algae)">Biological hydrogen production</a>. 95% of hydrogen is produced using natural gas,<sup id="cite_ref-Stupendous_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stupendous-72">[72]</a></sup> and 85% of hydrogen produced is used to remove sulfur from gasoline. Hydrogen can also be produced from <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a> by <a href="/wiki/Electrolysis" title="Electrolysis">electrolysis</a> at working efficiencies in the 50–60% range for the smaller electrolysers and around 65–70% for the larger plants.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> Hydrogen can also be made by chemical reduction using chemical hydrides or aluminum.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> Current technologies for manufacturing hydrogen use energy in various forms, totaling between 25 and 50 percent of the <a href="/wiki/Higher_heating_value" class="mw-redirect" title="Higher heating value">higher heating value</a> of the hydrogen fuel, used to produce, compress or liquefy, and transmit the hydrogen by pipeline or truck.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup></p>
<p>Environmental consequences of the production of hydrogen from fossil energy resources include the emission of <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gases" class="mw-redirect" title="Greenhouse gases">greenhouse gases</a>, a consequence that would also result from the on-board reforming of methanol into hydrogen.<sup id="cite_ref-EFCF_68-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EFCF-68">[68]</a></sup> Analyses comparing the environmental consequences of hydrogen production and use in fuel-cell vehicles to the refining of petroleum and combustion in conventional automobile engines do not agree on whether a net reduction of ozone and greenhouse gases would result.<sup id="cite_ref-Cox2014_7-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cox2014-7">[7]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Schultz_62-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schultz-62">[62]</a></sup> Hydrogen production using renewable energy resources would not create such emissions, but the scale of renewable energy production would need to be expanded to be used in producing hydrogen for a significant part of transportation needs.<sup id="cite_ref-DOE_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DOE-76">[76]</a></sup> As of 2015, 33 percent of U.S. electricity was produced from renewable sources (including 7% from hydro and 20% from nuclear).<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> In a few countries, renewable sources are being used more widely to produce energy and hydrogen. For example, <a href="/wiki/Iceland" title="Iceland">Iceland</a> is using <a href="/wiki/Geothermal_power" title="Geothermal power">geothermal power</a> to produce hydrogen,<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Denmark</a> is using <a href="/wiki/Wind_power" title="Wind power">wind</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Storage">Storage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydrogen_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Storage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">For more details on this topic, see <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_storage" title="Hydrogen storage">Hydrogen storage</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Blue_diamond_compressed_hydrogen.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Blue_diamond_compressed_hydrogen.jpg/170px-Blue_diamond_compressed_hydrogen.jpg" width="170" height="137" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/6b/Blue_diamond_compressed_hydrogen.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="183" data-file-height="148" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Blue_diamond_compressed_hydrogen.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Compressed hydrogen storage mark</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Hydrogen has a very low volumetric energy <a href="/wiki/Density" title="Density">density</a> at ambient conditions, equal to about one-third that of methane. Even when the fuel is stored as <a href="/wiki/Liquid_hydrogen" title="Liquid hydrogen">liquid hydrogen</a> in a <a href="/wiki/Cryogenic" class="mw-redirect" title="Cryogenic">cryogenic</a> tank or in a <a href="/wiki/Compressed_hydrogen" title="Compressed hydrogen">compressed hydrogen</a> <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_tank" title="Hydrogen tank">storage tank</a>, the volumetric energy density (megajoules per liter) is small relative to that of gasoline.<sup id="cite_ref-lanz_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lanz-16">[16]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-zubrin121_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-zubrin121-17">[17]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-olah155_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-olah155-18">[18]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-RSC2_55-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RSC2-55">[55]</a></sup> Hydrogen has a three times higher <a href="/wiki/Specific_energy" title="Specific energy">specific energy</a> by <a href="/wiki/Mass" title="Mass">mass</a> compared to gasoline (143 MJ/kg versus 46.9 MJ/kg). In 2011, scientists at <a href="/wiki/Los_Alamos_National_Laboratory" title="Los Alamos National Laboratory">Los Alamos National Laboratory</a> and <a href="/wiki/University_of_Alabama" title="University of Alabama">University of Alabama</a>, working with the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Department_of_Energy" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. Department of Energy">U.S. Department of Energy</a>, found a single-stage method for recharging ammonia borane, a hydrogen storage compound.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup></p>
<p>The DOE has studied hydrogen storage methods, focusing on on-board vehicular hydrogen storage systems that will allow for a driving range of 300+ miles.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> Compressed hydrogen in hydrogen tanks at 350 bar (5,000 psi) and 700 bar (10,000 psi) is used for hydrogen tank systems in vehicles, based on type IV carbon-composite technology.<sup id="cite_ref-store_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-store-83">[83]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Infrastructure">Infrastructure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydrogen_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Infrastructure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hydrogen_vehicle.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/68/Hydrogen_vehicle.jpg" width="200" height="138" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="200" data-file-height="138" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hydrogen_vehicle.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Hydrogen car fueling</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hydrogen_fueling_nozzle2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Hydrogen_fueling_nozzle2.jpg/220px-Hydrogen_fueling_nozzle2.jpg" width="220" height="161" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/cb/Hydrogen_fueling_nozzle2.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="304" data-file-height="223" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hydrogen_fueling_nozzle2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Hydrogen fueling</div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">For more details on this topic, see <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_infrastructure" title="Hydrogen infrastructure">Hydrogen infrastructure</a>.</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">For more details on this topic, see <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_highway" title="Hydrogen highway">Hydrogen highway</a>.</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_infrastructure" title="Hydrogen infrastructure">hydrogen infrastructure</a> consists mainly of industrial <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_pipeline_transport" title="Hydrogen pipeline transport">hydrogen pipeline transport</a> and hydrogen-equipped filling stations like those found on a <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_highway" title="Hydrogen highway">hydrogen highway</a>. <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_stations" class="mw-redirect" title="Hydrogen stations">Hydrogen stations</a> which are not situated near a hydrogen pipeline can obtain supply via <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_tank" title="Hydrogen tank">hydrogen tanks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Compressed_hydrogen_tube_trailer" title="Compressed hydrogen tube trailer">compressed hydrogen tube trailers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Liquid_hydrogen_tank_truck" class="mw-redirect" title="Liquid hydrogen tank truck">liquid hydrogen tank trucks</a> or dedicated onsite production.</p>
<p>According to GM, 70% of the U.S. population lives near a hydrogen-generating facility but has little access to hydrogen, despite its wide availability for commercial use.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> The distribution of hydrogen fuel for vehicles throughout the U.S. would require new hydrogen stations that would cost, by some estimates approximately 20 billion dollars<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> and 4.6 billion in the EU.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> Other estimates place the cost as high as half trillion dollars in the United States alone.<sup id="cite_ref-Cox2014_7-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cox2014-7">[7]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup></p>
<p>As of 2016<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Hydrogen_vehicle&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, there are 23 publicly accessible <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_station" title="Hydrogen station">hydrogen refueling stations</a> in the USA, 20 of which are <a href="/wiki/California_Hydrogen_Highway" title="California Hydrogen Highway">located in California</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-afdc_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-afdc-88">[88]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> They are mainly sourced by natural gas.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Codes_and_standards">Codes and standards</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydrogen_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Codes and standards">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydrogen_codes_and_standards&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Hydrogen codes and standards (page does not exist)">Hydrogen codes and standards</a>, as well as codes and technical standards for <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_safety" title="Hydrogen safety">hydrogen safety</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_storage" title="Hydrogen storage">storage of hydrogen</a>, have been identified as an institutional barrier to deploying <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_technologies" title="Hydrogen technologies">hydrogen technologies</a> and developing a <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_economy" title="Hydrogen economy">hydrogen economy</a>. To enable the commercialization of hydrogen in consumer products, new codes and standards must be developed and adopted by federal, state and local governments.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Official_support">Official support</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydrogen_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Official support">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="US_initiatives">US initiatives</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydrogen_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: US initiatives">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 2003, <a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">George W. Bush</a> announced an initiative to promote hydrogen powered vehicles.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> In 2009, President Obama and his Department of Energy Secretary <a href="/wiki/Steven_Chu" title="Steven Chu">Steven Chu</a> stripped the funding of fuel cell technology due to their belief that the technology was still decades away. Under heavy criticism, the funding was partially restored.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> In 2014 the Obama administration announced they want to speed up production and development of hydrogen powered vehicles. The Department of Energy planned to spread a $7.2 million investment among the states of Georgia, Kansas, Pennsylvania, and Tennessee to support projects that fuel vehicles and support power systems. The Center for Transportation and The Environment, Fed Ex Express, Air Products and Chemicals, and Sprint have invested in the development of fuel cells. Fuel cells could also be used in handling equipment such as forklifts as well as telecommunications infrastructure.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Byron_L._Dorgan" class="mw-redirect" title="Byron L. Dorgan">Senator Byron L. Dorgan</a> stated in 2013: “The Energy and Water Appropriations bill makes investments in our nation’s efforts to develop safe, homegrown energy sources that will reduce our reliance on foreign oil. And, because ongoing research and development is necessary to develop game-changing technologies, this bill also restores funding for Hydrogen energy research”. In June 2013, the U.S. Department of Energy gave 9 million dollars in grants to speed up technology development, 4.5 million for advanced fuel cell membranes, $3 million to 3M corporation to work on membranes with improved durability and performance, and 1.5 million to the Colorado School of Mines for work on simpler and more affordable fuel cell membranes.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_efforts">Other efforts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydrogen_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Other efforts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In Japan, hydrogen is mainly to be sourced from outside Japan.<sup id="cite_ref-zmorris_61-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-zmorris-61">[61]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup></p>
<p>Norway plans a series of hydrogen refueling stations along the main roads.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Criticism">Criticism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydrogen_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Criticism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Critics claim the time frame for overcoming the technical and economic challenges to implementing wide-scale use of hydrogen cars is likely to last for at least several decades, and hydrogen vehicles may never become broadly available.<sup id="cite_ref-TechRev_67-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TechRev-67">[67]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Meyers1_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meyers1-100">[100]</a></sup> They claim that the focus on the use of the hydrogen car is a dangerous detour from more readily available solutions to reducing the use of fossil fuels in vehicles.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> In May 2008, <i><a href="/wiki/Wired_News" class="mw-redirect" title="Wired News">Wired News</a></i> reported that "experts say it will be 40 years or more before hydrogen has any meaningful impact on gasoline consumption or global warming, and we can't afford to wait that long. In the meantime, fuel cells are diverting resources from more immediate solutions."<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup></p>
<p>K. G. Duleep commented that "a strong case exists for continuing <a href="/wiki/Fuel-efficiency" class="mw-redirect" title="Fuel-efficiency">fuel-efficiency</a> improvements from conventional technology at relatively low cost."<sup id="cite_ref-Boyd_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Boyd-103">[103]</a></sup> Critiques of hydrogen vehicles are presented in the 2006 documentary, <i><a href="/wiki/Who_Killed_the_Electric_Car%3F" title="Who Killed the Electric Car?">Who Killed the Electric Car?</a></i>. According to former <a href="/wiki/U.S._Department_of_Energy" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. Department of Energy">U.S. Department of Energy</a> official <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Romm" class="mw-redirect" title="Joseph Romm">Joseph Romm</a>, "A hydrogen car is one of the least efficient, most expensive ways to reduce greenhouse gases." Asked when hydrogen cars will be broadly available, Romm replied: "Not in our lifetime, and very possibly never."<sup id="cite_ref-Boyd_103-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Boyd-103">[103]</a></sup> The <i>Los Angeles Times</i> wrote, in February 2009, "Hydrogen fuel-cell technology won't work in cars. ... Any way you look at it, hydrogen is a lousy way to move cars."<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup></p>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/The_Economist" title="The Economist">The Economist</a></i> magazine, in September 2008, quoted <a href="/wiki/Robert_Zubrin" title="Robert Zubrin">Robert Zubrin</a>, the author of <i><a href="/wiki/Energy_Victory" title="Energy Victory">Energy Victory</a></i>, as saying: "Hydrogen is 'just about the worst possible vehicle fuel'".<sup id="cite_ref-Economist_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Economist-105">[105]</a></sup> The magazine noted the withdrawal of California from earlier goals: "In March [2008] the <a href="/wiki/California_Air_Resources_Board" title="California Air Resources Board">California Air Resources Board</a>, an agency of California's state government and a bellwether for state governments across America, changed its requirement for the number of <a href="/wiki/Zero-emission_vehicle" class="mw-redirect" title="Zero-emission vehicle">zero-emission vehicles</a> (ZEVs) to be built and sold in California between 2012 and 2014. The revised mandate allows manufacturers to comply with the rules by building more battery-electric cars instead of fuel-cell vehicles."<sup id="cite_ref-Economist_105-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Economist-105">[105]</a></sup> The magazine also noted that most hydrogen is produced through steam reformation, which creates at least as much emission of carbon per mile as some of today's gasoline cars. On the other hand, if the hydrogen could be produced using renewable energy, "it would surely be easier simply to use this energy to charge the batteries of all-electric or plug-in hybrid vehicles."<sup id="cite_ref-Economist_105-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Economist-105">[105]</a></sup></p>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/The_Washington_Post" title="The Washington Post">The Washington Post</a></i> asked in November 2009, "But why would you want to store energy in the form of hydrogen and then use that hydrogen to produce electricity for a motor, when electrical energy is already waiting to be sucked out of sockets all over America and stored in auto batteries"?.<sup id="cite_ref-Stupendous_72-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stupendous-72">[72]</a></sup> A December 2009 study at <a href="/wiki/University_of_California,_Davis" title="University of California, Davis">UC Davis</a>, published in the <i>Journal of Power Sources</i>, found that, over their lifetimes, hydrogen vehicles will emit more carbon than gasoline vehicles.<sup id="cite_ref-UCDavis_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UCDavis-106">[106]</a></sup> This agrees with a 2014 analysis.<sup id="cite_ref-Cox2014_7-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cox2014-7">[7]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/The_Motley_Fool" title="The Motley Fool">The Motley Fool</a></i> stated in 2013 that "there are still cost-prohibitive obstacles [for hydrogen cars] relating to transportation, storage, and, most importantly, production."<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup></p>
<p>Volkswagen's Rudolf Krebs said in 2013 that "no matter how excellent you make the cars themselves, the laws of physics hinder their overall efficiency. The most efficient way to convert energy to mobility is electricity." He elaborated: "Hydrogen mobility only makes sense if you use green energy", but ... you need to convert it first into hydrogen "with low efficiencies" where "you lose about 40 percent of the initial energy". You then must compress the hydrogen and store it under high pressure in tanks, which uses more energy. "And then you have to convert the hydrogen back to electricity in a fuel cell with another efficiency loss". Krebs continued: "in the end, from your original 100 percent of electric energy, you end up with 30 to 40 percent."<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/The_Business_Insider" class="mw-redirect" title="The Business Insider">The Business Insider</a></i> commented:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>Pure hydrogen can be industrially derived, but it takes energy. If that energy does not come from renewable sources, then fuel-cell cars are not as clean as they seem. ... Another challenge is the lack of infrastructure. Gas stations need to invest in the ability to refuel hydrogen tanks before FCEVs become practical, and it's unlikely many will do that while there are so few customers on the road today. ... Compounding the lack of infrastructure is the high cost of the technology. Fuel cells are "still very, very expensive".<sup id="cite_ref-Davies1_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Davies1-109">[109]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>In 2014, Joseph Romm devoted three articles to updating his critiques of hydrogen vehicles. He states that FCVs still have not overcome the following issues: high cost of the vehicles, high fueling cost, and a lack of fuel-delivery infrastructure. "It would take several miracles to overcome all of those problems simultaneously in the coming decades."<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> Most importantly, he says, "FCVs aren't green" because of escaping methane during natural gas extraction and when hydrogen is produced, as 95% of it is, using the steam reforming process. He concludes that renewable energy cannot economically be used to make hydrogen for an FCV fleet "either now or in the future."<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/GreenTech_Media" class="mw-redirect" title="GreenTech Media">GreenTech Media</a>'s analyst reached similar conclusions in 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> In 2015, <i>Clean Technica</i> listed some of the disadvantages of hydrogen fuel cell vehicles.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> So did <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Car_Throttle&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Car Throttle (page does not exist)">Car Throttle</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> Another <i>Clean Technica</i> writer concluded, "while hydrogen may have a part to play in the world of energy storage (especially seasonal storage), it looks like a dead end when it comes to mainstream vehicles."<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> A 2016 study in the November issue of the journal <i><a href="/wiki/Energy_(journal)" title="Energy (journal)">Energy</a></i> by scientists at <a href="/wiki/Stanford_University" title="Stanford University">Stanford University</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Technical_University_of_Munich" title="Technical University of Munich">Technical University of Munich</a> concluded that, even assuming local hydrogen production,"investing in all-electric battery vehicles is a more economical choice for reducing carbon dioxide emissions, primarily due to their lower cost and significantly higher energy efficiency."<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Comparison_with_other_types_of_alternative_fuel_vehicle">Comparison with other types of alternative fuel vehicle</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydrogen_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Comparison with other types of alternative fuel vehicle">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Hydrogen vehicles compete with various proposed alternatives to the modern <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">fossil fuel</a> powered vehicle infrastructure.<sup id="cite_ref-RSC2_55-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RSC2-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Plug-in_hybrids">Plug-in hybrids</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydrogen_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Plug-in hybrids">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Update" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div style="width:52px"><img alt="Ambox current red.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Ambox_current_red.svg/42px-Ambox_current_red.svg.png" width="42" height="34" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Ambox_current_red.svg/63px-Ambox_current_red.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Ambox_current_red.svg/84px-Ambox_current_red.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="360" data-file-height="290" /></div>
</td>
<td class="mbox-text"><span class="mbox-text-span">This section needs to be <b>updated</b>. <span class="hide-when-compact">Please update this article to reflect recent events or newly available information.<br />
<small>Last update: No content is presented about series production PHEVs launched starting with the <a href="/wiki/BYD_F3DM" title="BYD F3DM">BYD F3DM</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Chevrolet_Volt" title="Chevrolet Volt">Chevrolet Volt</a>. Figures presented do not reflect current technology available.</small></span> <small><i>(November 2013)</i></small></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Plug-in_hybrid" title="Plug-in hybrid">Plug-in hybrid</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Plug-in_hybrid_electric_vehicle" class="mw-redirect" title="Plug-in hybrid electric vehicle">Plug-in hybrid electric vehicles</a>, or PHEVs, are <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_vehicle" title="Hybrid vehicle">hybrid vehicles</a> that can be plugged into the electric grid and contain an electric motor and also an <a href="/wiki/Internal_combustion_engine" title="Internal combustion engine">internal combustion engine</a>. The PHEV concept augments standard <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_electric_vehicles" class="mw-redirect" title="Hybrid electric vehicles">hybrid electric vehicles</a> with the ability to recharge their batteries from an external source, enabling increased use of the vehicle's electric motors while reducing their reliance on internal combustion engines. The infrastructure required to charge PHEVs is already in place,<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> and transmission of power from grid to car is about 93% efficient.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> This, however, is not the only energy loss in transferring power from grid to wheels. AC/DC conversion must take place from the grids AC supply to the PHEV's DC. This is roughly 98% efficient.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> The battery then must be charged. As of 2007, the <a href="/wiki/Lithium_iron_phosphate_battery" title="Lithium iron phosphate battery">Lithium iron phosphate battery</a> was between 80-90% efficient in charging/discharging.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> The battery needs to be cooled; the GM Volt's battery has 4 coolers and two radiators.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> As of 2009, "the total well-to-wheels efficiency with which a hydrogen fuel cell vehicle might utilize renewable electricity is roughly 20% (although that number could rise to 25% or a little higher with the kind of multiple technology breakthroughs required to enable a hydrogen economy). The well-to-wheels efficiency of charging an onboard battery and then discharging it to run an electric motor in a PHEV or EV, however, is 80% (and could be higher in the future)—four times more efficient than current hydrogen fuel cell vehicle pathways."<sup id="cite_ref-EnergyCollective_70-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EnergyCollective-70">[70]</a></sup> A 2006 article in <i><a href="/wiki/Scientific_American" title="Scientific American">Scientific American</a></i> argued that PHEVs, rather than hydrogen vehicles, would become standard in the automobile industry.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> A December 2009 study at UC Davis found that, over their lifetimes, PHEVs will emit less carbon than current vehicles, while hydrogen cars will emit more carbon than gasoline vehicles.<sup id="cite_ref-UCDavis_106-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UCDavis-106">[106]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Natural_gas">Natural gas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydrogen_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Natural gas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas_vehicle" title="Natural gas vehicle">Natural gas vehicle</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Internal_combustion_engine" title="Internal combustion engine">ICE</a>-based <a href="/wiki/Compressed_natural_gas" title="Compressed natural gas">CNG</a>, <a href="/wiki/HCNG" title="HCNG">HCNG</a> or <a href="/wiki/Liquefied_natural_gas" title="Liquefied natural gas">LNG</a> vehicles (<a href="/wiki/Natural_gas_vehicle" title="Natural gas vehicle">Natural gas vehicles</a> or NGVs) use methane (<a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">Natural gas</a> or <a href="/wiki/Biogas" title="Biogas">Biogas</a>) directly as a fuel source. Natural gas has a higher <a href="/wiki/Energy_density" title="Energy density">energy density</a> than hydrogen gas. NGVs using biogas are nearly <a href="/wiki/Carbon_neutral" class="mw-redirect" title="Carbon neutral">carbon neutral</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> Unlike hydrogen vehicles, CNG vehicles have been available for many years, and there is sufficient infrastructure to provide both commercial and home refueling stations. Worldwide, there were 14.8 million natural gas vehicles by the end of 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="All-electric_vehicles">All-electric vehicles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydrogen_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: All-electric vehicles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Electric_car" title="Electric car">electric car</a></div>
<p>A 2008 <i>Technology Review</i> article stated, "Electric cars—and plug-in hybrid cars—have an enormous advantage over hydrogen fuel-cell vehicles in utilizing low-carbon electricity. That is because of the inherent inefficiency of the entire hydrogen fueling process, from generating the hydrogen with that electricity to transporting this diffuse gas long distances, getting the hydrogen in the car, and then running it through a fuel cell—all for the purpose of converting the hydrogen back into electricity to drive the same exact electric motor you'll find in an electric car."<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> Thermodynamically, each additional step in the conversion process decreases the overall efficiency of the process.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup></p>
<p>A 2013 comparison of hydrogen and <a href="/wiki/Battery_electric_vehicle" title="Battery electric vehicle">battery electric vehicles</a> agreed with the 25% figure from Ulf Bossel in 2006 and stated that the cost of an <a href="/wiki/Electric_vehicle_battery" title="Electric vehicle battery">electric vehicle battery</a> "is rapidly coming down, and the gap will widen further", while there is little "existing infrastructure to transport, store and deliver hydrogen to vehicles and would cost billions of dollars to put into place, everyone's household power sockets are "electric vehicle refueling" station and the "cost of electricity (depending on the source) is at least 75% cheaper than hydrogen."<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> In 2013 the <a href="/wiki/National_Academy_of_Sciences" title="National Academy of Sciences">National Academy of Sciences</a><sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup> and DOE stated that even under optimistic conditions by 2030 the price for the battery is not expected to go below $17,000 ($200–$250/kWh) on 300 miles of range.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup> In 2013 Matthew Mench, from the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Tennessee" title="University of Tennessee">University of Tennessee</a> stated "If we are sitting around waiting for a battery breakthrough that will give us four times the range than we have now, we are going to be waiting for a long time".<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> Navigant Research, (formerly Pike research), on the other hand, forecasts that “lithium-ion costs, which are tipping the scales at about $500 per kilowatt hour now, could fall to $300 by 2015 and to $180 by 2020.”<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup> In 2013 Takeshi Uchiyamada, a designer of the <a href="/wiki/Toyota_Prius" title="Toyota Prius">Toyota Prius</a> stated: "Because of its shortcomings – driving range, cost and recharging time – the electric vehicle is not a viable replacement for most conventional cars".<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup></p>
<p>Many <a href="/wiki/Electric_car" title="Electric car">electric car</a> designs offer limited driving range causing <a href="/wiki/Range_anxiety" title="Range anxiety">range anxiety</a>. For example, the 2013 <a href="/wiki/Nissan_Leaf" title="Nissan Leaf">Nissan Leaf</a> has a range of 75&#160;mi (121&#160;km),<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup> the 2014 <a href="/wiki/Mercedes-Benz_B-Class_Electric_Drive" class="mw-redirect" title="Mercedes-Benz B-Class Electric Drive">Mercedes-Benz B-Class Electric Drive</a> has an estimated range of 115&#160;mi (185&#160;km)<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Tesla_Model_S" title="Tesla Model S">Tesla Model S</a> has a range of up to 265&#160;mi (426&#160;km).<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup> However, most US commutes are 30–40 miles (48–64&#160;km) miles per day round trip<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup> and in Europe most commutes are around 20 kilometres (12&#160;mi) round-trip<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2013, <i>The New York Times</i> stated that there are only 10 publicly accessible hydrogen filling stations in the U.S., eight of which are in Southern California, and that BEVs' cost-per-mile expense in 2013 is one-third as much as hydrogen cars, when comparing electricity from the grid and hydrogen at a filling station. The <i>Times</i> commented: "By the time Toyota sells its first fuel-cell sedan, there will be about a half-million plug-in vehicles on the road in the United States – and tens of thousands of E.V. charging stations."<sup id="cite_ref-Berman2013_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Berman2013-5">[5]</a></sup> In 2013 John Swanton of the <a href="/wiki/California_Air_Resources_Board" title="California Air Resources Board">California Air Resources Board</a>, who sees them as complementary technologies, stated that EVs have the jump on fuel-cell autos, which "are like electric vehicles were 10 years ago. EVs are for real consumers, no strings attached. With EVs you have a lot of infrastructure in place.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/The_Business_Insider" class="mw-redirect" title="The Business Insider">The Business Insider</a></i>, in 2013 commented that if the energy to produce hydrogen "does not come from renewable sources, then fuel-cell cars are not as clean as they seem. ... Gas stations need to invest in the ability to refuel hydrogen tanks before FCEVs become practical, and it's unlikely many will do that while there are so few customers on the road today. ... Compounding the lack of infrastructure is the high cost of the technology. Fuel cells are "still very, very expensive", even compared to battery-powered EVs.<sup id="cite_ref-Davies1_109-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Davies1-109">[109]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydrogen_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="reflist columns references-column-count references-column-count-2" style="-moz-column-count: 2; -webkit-column-count: 2; column-count: 2; list-style-type: decimal;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-1">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://transportevolved.com/2014/06/25/toyota-unveils-2015-fuel-cell-sedan-will-retail-japan-around-%C2%A57-million/">"Toyota Unveils 2015 Fuel Cell Sedan, Will Retail in Japan For Around ¥7 Million"</a>. transportevolved.com. 2014-06-25<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">2014-06-26</span></span>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHydrogen+vehicle&amp;rft.btitle=Toyota+Unveils+2015+Fuel+Cell+Sedan%2C+Will+Retail+in+Japan+For+Around+%A57+Million&amp;rft.date=2014-06-25&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Ftransportevolved.com%2F2014%2F06%2F25%2Ftoyota-unveils-2015-fuel-cell-sedan-will-retail-japan-around-%25C2%25A57-million%2F&amp;rft.pub=transportevolved.com&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span></span></li>

			<div id="mw-head">
									<div id="p-personal" role="navigation" class="" aria-labelledby="p-personal-label">
